240 if (this.registered.length === this.scripts.length) {

241 this.registered.forEach(function (fn) {

242 if (typeof fn === 'function') {

243 setTimeout(fn, 0);

244 }

245 });

246 this.ready = function () {};

247 }

248 },

249

250 insert: function (s) {

251 var t = d.getElementById('js-position' + this.index);

252 if (t && t.insertAdjacentElement) {

253 t.insertAdjacentElement('afterend', s);

254 } else {

255 d.head.appendChild(s);

256 }

257 ++this.index;

258 },

259

260 createScript: function (script, beforeLoad) {

261 var s = d.createElement('script');

262 s.id = 'js-position' + (this.index + 1);

263 s.setAttribute('data-test', script.test);

264 if (beforeLoad) {

265 s.defer = 'defer';

266 s.onload = function () {

267 if (script.noinit) {

268 loader.register(true);

269 }

270 if (d.readyState === 'interactive' || d.readyState === 'complete') {

271 loader.ready();

272 }

273 };

274 } else {

275 s.async = 'async';

276 }

277 s.src = script.src;

278 return s;

279 },

280

281 init: function () {

282 this.scripts.forEach(function (s) {

283 loader.insert(loader.createScript(s, true));

284 });

285

286 d.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {

287 loader.ready();

288

289 var conditionalScripts = [

290 {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es6-bundle-464a2af269.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js', module: true },

291 {match: 'div[data-pan-container]', src: '/static/js/pan-zoom-es5-bundle-2626f1bdf8.js', test: 'pan-zoom-js', nomodule: true },

292 {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es6-bundle-d616f8e4f9.js', test: 'math-js', module: true},

293 {match: 'math,span.mathjax-tex', src: '/static/js/math-es5-bundle-66109c5184.js', test: 'math-js', nomodule: true}

294 ];

295

296

297 if (conditionalScripts) {

298 conditionalScripts.filter(function (script) {

299 return !!document.querySelector(script.match) && !((moduleSupport && script.nomodule) || (!moduleSupport && script.module));

300 }).forEach(function (script) {

301 loader.insert(loader.createScript(script));

302 });

303 }

304 }, false);

305 }

306 };

307 loader.init();

308 }

309 })(window, document);

310</script>

356 <meta name="dc.description" content="Metastases are initiated by disseminated tumor cells (DTCs) that colonize distant organs. Growing evidence suggests that the microenvironment of the primary tumor primes DTCs for dormant or proliferative fates. However, the manner in which this occurs remains poorly understood. Here, using the Window for High-Resolution Intravital Imaging of the Lung (WHRIL), we study the live lung longitudinally and follow the fate of individual DTCs that spontaneously disseminate from orthotopic breast tumors. We find that spontaneously DTCs have increased levels of retention, increased speed of extravasation, and greater survival after extravasation, compared to experimentally metastasized tumor cells. Detailed analysis reveals that a subset of macrophages within the primary tumor induces a pro-dissemination and pro-dormancy DTC phenotype. Our work provides insight into how specific primary tumor microenvironments prime a subpopulation of cells for expression of proteins associated with dissemination and dormancy. The understanding of the mechanisms underlying the ability of disseminated tumor cells (DTCs) to form metastasis is incomplete. Here, by using high-resolution intravital imaging of the murine lung to track the fate of breast-derived DTCs, the authors show that macrophages within the primary tumor induce a pro-dissemination and pro-dormancy phenotype in tumor cells, favouring their extravasation in the lung."/>

Here, using the Window for High-Resolution Intravital Imaging of the Lung (WHRIL), we study the live lung longitudinally and follow the fate of individual DTCs that spontaneously disseminate from orthotopic breast tumors. Here, by using high-resolution intravital imaging of the murine lung to track the fate of breast-derived DTCs, the authors show that macrophages within the primary tumor induce a pro-dissemination and pro-dormancy phenotype in tumor cells, favouring their extravasation in the lung."/>

941

942 <div class="c-article-body">

943 <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Metastases are initiated by disseminated tumor cells (DTCs) that colonize distant organs. Growing evidence suggests that the microenvironment of the primary tumor primes DTCs for dormant or proliferative fates. However, the manner in which this occurs remains poorly understood. Here, using the Window for High-Resolution Intravital Imaging of the Lung (WHRIL), we study the live lung longitudinally and follow the fate of individual DTCs that spontaneously disseminate from orthotopic breast tumors. We find that spontaneously DTCs have increased levels of retention, increased speed of extravasation, and greater survival after extravasation, compared to experimentally metastasized tumor cells. Detailed analysis reveals that a subset of macrophages within the primary tumor induces a pro-dissemination and pro-dormancy DTC phenotype. Our work provides insight into how specific primary tumor microenvironments prime a subpopulation of cells for expression of proteins associated with dissemination and dormancy.</p></div></div></section>

944 <noscript>

945

946 </noscript>

947

950 <div class="main-content">

951 <section data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Metastasis causes approximately 90% of cancer-related mortality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Mehlen, P. &amp; Puisieux, A. Metastasis: a question of life or death. Nat. Rev. Cancer 6, 449458 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e761">1</a></sup>. It is the endpoint of a complex and dynamic cascade of steps in which tumor cells migrate within the primary tumor, intravasate, disseminate via the circulatory system, arrest at a secondary site, extravasate, survive, and finally re-initiate growth to form secondary tumors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Lambert, A. W., Pattabiraman, D. R. &amp; Weinberg, R. A. Emerging Biological Principles of Metastasis. Cell 168, 670691 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e765">2</a></sup>. Most therapeutic interventions targeting metastasis are designed only to attack actively dividing tumor cells (i.e., those in the last step of this cascade; active growth) and do not block any of the intermediate steps. Understanding the mechanisms that regulate the ability of disseminated tumor cells (DTCs) to complete all steps of metastasis will reveal additional targets for novel anti-metastatic therapeutics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zijlstra, A. et al. The importance of developing therapies targeting the biological spectrum of metastatic disease. Clin. Exp. Metastasis 36, 305309 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e769">3</a></sup>.</p><p>Since 1900, a number of studies have attempted to measure the fate of DTCs during the metastatic cascade using a variety of techniques. These include histopathology of secondary sites such as the lung<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Levin, I. &amp; Sittenfield, M. J. On the Mechanism of the Formation of Metastases in Malignant Tumors: An Experimental Study. J. Exp. Med. 14, 148158 (1911)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e776">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Spiegel, A. et al. Neutrophils Suppress Intraluminal NK Cell-Mediated Tumor Cell Clearance and Enhance Extravasation of Disseminated Carcinoma Cells. Cancer Discov. 6, 630649 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e779">5</a></sup>; radioactive labeling and fate-mapping of DTCs in mice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Baserga, R., Kisieleski, W. E. &amp; Halvorsen, K. A study on the establishment and growth of tumor metastases with tritiated thymidine. Cancer Res. 20, 910917 (1960)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e783">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fidler, I. J. Metastasis: quantitative analysis of distribution and fate of tumor emboli labeled with 125 I-5-iodo-2-deoxyuridine. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 45, 773782 (1970)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e786">7</a></sup>; ex vivo visualization of DTCs in tissue explants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Al-Mehdi, A. B. et al. Intravascular origin of metastasis from the proliferation of endothelium-attached tumor cells: a new model for metastasis. Nat. Med. 6, 100102 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e790">8</a></sup>; and in vivo visualization of DTCs in the Chick Chorioallantoic Membrane (CAM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="MacDonald, I. C., Schmidt, E. E., Morris, V. L., Chambers, A. F. &amp; Groom, A. C. Intravital videomicroscopy of the chorioallantoic microcirculation: a model system for studying metastasis. Microvasc Res. 44, 185199 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e794">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Koop, S. et al. Fate of melanoma cells entering the microcirculation: over 80% survive and extravasate. Cancer Res. 55, 25202523 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e797">10</a></sup> and in secondary sites in mice such as the liver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Naumov, G. N. et al. Cellular expression of green fluorescent protein, coupled with high- resolution in vivo videomicroscopy, to monitor steps in tumor metastasis. J. Cell Sci. 112, 18351842 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e801">11</a></sup>, brain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kienast, Y. et al. Real-time imaging reveals the single steps of brain metastasis formation. Nat. Med. 16, 116122 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e806">12</a></sup>, and lung<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Entenberg, D. et al. In vivo subcellular resolution optical imaging in the lung reveals early metastatic proliferation and motility. Intravital 4, 111 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e810">13</a></sup>. However, the results of these studies remain contradictory, alternatively identifying tumor cell survival in the circulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fidler, I. J. Metastasis: quantitative analysis of distribution and fate of tumor emboli labeled with 125 I-5-iodo-2-deoxyuridine. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 45, 773782 (1970)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e814">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Weiss, L. Biomechanical destruction of cancer cells in skeletal muscle: a rate-regulator for hematogenous metastasis. Clin. Exp. Metastasis 7, 483491 (1989)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e817">14</a></sup>, extravasation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Liotta, L. A., Steeg, P. S. &amp; Stetler-Stevenson, W. G. Cancer metastasis and angiogenesis: an imbalance of positive and negative regulation. Cell 64, 327336 (1991)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e821">15</a></sup>, and re-initiation of growth/initiation of dormancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Luzzi, K. J. et al. Multistep nature of metastatic inefficiency: dormancy of solitary cells after successful extravasation and limited survival of early micrometastases. Am. J. Pathol. 153, 865873 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e825">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Cameron, M. D. et al. Temporal progression of metastasis in lung: cell survival, dormancy, and location dependence of metastatic inefficiency. Cancer Res. 60, 25412546 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e828">17</a></sup> as rate-limiting steps.</p><p>Furthermore, the vast majority of these studies have relied on a technique called experimental metastasis (EM). EM is a process in which tumor cells are injected directly into the vasculature of mice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Levin, I. &amp; Sittenfield, M. J. On the Mechanism of the Formation of Metastases in Malignant Tumors: An Experimental Study. J. Exp. Med. 14, 148158 (1911)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e835">4</a></sup> and is used in place of spontaneous metastasis (SM), where tumor cells in an orthotopic primary tumor stochastically progress through all of the steps of the metastatic cascade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Levin, I. &amp; Sittenfield, M. J. On the Mechanism of the Formation of Metastases in Malignant Tumors: An Experimental Study. J. Exp. Med. 14, 148158 (1911)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e839">4</a></sup>. EM assays assume that tumor cells injected as a bolus directly into the vasculature will have the same fate (and provide the same information on metastasis initiation) as do tumor cells which originate from within a primary tumor.</p><p>However, a growing literature shows that the presence of a primary tumor can have a profound influence on the metastatic outcome. For example, it has been determined that gene expression signatures in the primary tumor can provide information on the potential for metastatic relapse years after the primary tumor has been removed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sosa, M. S., Bragado, P., Debnath, J. &amp; Aguirre-Ghiso, J. A. Regulation of tumor cell dormancy by tissue microenvironments and autophagy. Adv. Exp. Med. Biol. 734, 7389 (2013)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e846">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cheng, Q. et al. A signature of epithelial-mesenchymal plasticity and stromal activation in primary tumor modulates late recurrence in breast cancer independent of disease subtype. Breast Cancer Res. 16, 407 (2014)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e846_1">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS One 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e849">20</a></sup>. Furthermore, specific primary tumor microenvironments, such as hypoxia, can prime those primary tumor cells that are destined to become DTCs to have divergent fates in target organs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e853">21</a></sup>. Despite these advances, it is not known what influence the primary tumor has on the early steps of the metastatic cascade in the secondary site.</p><p>In the past, we, and others, have used high-resolution intravital imaging (IVI) to investigate the process of intravasation and dissemination in the primary tumor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Borriello, L. et al. The role of the tumor microenvironment in tumor cell intravasation and dissemination. Eur. J. Cell Biol 99, 151098 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e861">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Coste, A., Oktay, M. H., Condeelis, J. S. &amp; Entenberg, D. Intravital Imaging Techniques for Biomedical and Clinical Research. Cytometry A. 97, 448457 (2020)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e864">23</a></sup>. To understand the influence the primary tumor has on spontaneously disseminating tumor cells in the secondary site, we recently developed the implantable Window for High-Resolution Imaging of the Lung (WHRIL)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Entenberg, D. et al. A permanent window for the murine lung enables high-resolution imaging of cancer metastasis. Nat. Methods 15, 7380 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e868">24</a></sup>. Unlike prior techniques, the WHRIL is capable of providing a longitudinal view of the living lung at subcellular resolution over days to weeks, making it possible to follow, quantitatively, the fate of individual disseminated tumor cells throughout the process of colonization.</p><p>To our knowledge, spontaneously disseminating tumor cells have never been quantitatively measured, in real-time, during the process of metastasis to the lung. Thus, we here employed this tool to directly and longitudinally visualize the secondary site with single-cell resolution IVI throughout the process of spontaneous metastasis in order to directly measure the primary tumors influence on the initial steps of the metastatic cascade in the secondary site. We find that, compared to intravascularly injected tumor cells, spontaneously DTCs have increased levels of retention, extravasate from the lung vasculature more quickly, and have a greater rates of survival after extravasation. Our investigation into the mechanism underlying these phenotypes reveals that a subset of macrophages within the primary tumor activates programs of dissemination and dormancy in DTCs as they approach intravasation sites.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Real-time observation of tumor cell arrival to the lung</h3><p>We began our investigation by generating mouse models of spontaneous metastasis. For these studies, we specifically chose to use orthotopic injections of cancer cell lines, and not transgenic animal models of cancer, so as to be able adhere closely to earlier studies of metastasis, which all used cancer cell lines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fidler, I. J. Metastasis: quantitative analysis of distribution and fate of tumor emboli labeled with 125 I-5-iodo-2-deoxyuridine. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 45, 773782 (1970)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e887">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liotta, L. A., Steeg, P. S. &amp; Stetler-Stevenson, W. G. Cancer metastasis and angiogenesis: an imbalance of positive and negative regulation. Cell 64, 327336 (1991)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e890">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Luzzi, K. J. et al. Multistep nature of metastatic inefficiency: dormancy of solitary cells after successful extravasation and limited survival of early micrometastases. Am. J. Pathol. 153, 865873 (1998)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e890_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Cameron, M. D. et al. Temporal progression of metastasis in lung: cell survival, dormancy, and location dependence of metastatic inefficiency. Cancer Res. 60, 25412546 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e893">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hofer, K. G., Prensky, W. &amp; Hughes, W. L. Death and metastatic distribution of tumor cells in mice monitored with 125I-iododeoxy-uridine. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 43, 763773 (1969)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e896">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Morris, V. L. et al. Mammary carcinoma cell lines of high and low metastatic potential differ not in extravasation but in subsequent migration and growth. Clin. Exp. Metastasis 12, 357367 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e899">26</a></sup>. Specifically, we developed orthotopic primary breast tumors in mice using E0771 cells labeled with green fluorescent protein (E0771-GFP) and grown in syngeneic C57/B6J VeCad-tdTomato mice (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1a</a>, top) or, to ensure that the observed biological findings were generalizable, using human tumor cells (MDA-MB-231) labeled with GFP (231-GFP) grown in nude immunocompromised mice. To observe DTCs in the lung, we implanted the WHRIL into tumor-bearing mice, and 24 hrs later, imaged them with intravital imaging (IVI) using multiphoton microscopy (Supplementary Movie<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM2">1</a>), as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Entenberg, D. et al. A permanent window for the murine lung enables high-resolution imaging of cancer metastasis. Nat. Methods 15, 7380 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e909">24</a></sup>. Using this method, we were able to visualize both intravascular, extravascular tumor cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">1a, b</a>, Supplementary Movies<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2</a> &amp; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM4">3</a>), and pre-existing metastases, and to observe the arrival of new DTCs to the lung vasculature in real time (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1b</a>, Arrival, top and bottom).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Tumor cells that spontaneously disseminate from the primary tumor to the lung have a drastically increased metastatic efficiency compared to intravenously injected tumor cells."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Tumor cells that spontaneously disseminate from the primary tumor to the lung have a drastically increased metastatic efficiency compared to intravenously injected tumor cells.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig1" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure 1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="511"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Experimental design to track the fate of individual disseminated tumor cells using an Experimental Metastasis (EM) model (top) and a Spontaneous Metastasis (SM) model (bottom). <b>b</b> Serial imaging through the WHRIL allows tracking of the fate of disseminated tumor cells. Vasculature before arrival (left), upon arrival (middle) and after arrival of tumor cells (TCs). Fate of TCs (yellow arrows) could be either recirculation or apoptosis (right, top) or extravasation into the lung parenchyma (right, bottom). Red=tdTomato labeled endothelial cells and 155kDa Tetramethylrhodamine dextran labeled blood serum, Green=GFP labeled tumor cells, Blue=second harmonic generation. Scale bar=15m. <b>c</b> Kaplan-Meier survival curves showing the percentage of E0771-GFP tumor cells observed under the WHRIL at each 8hr time point over a period of 64 hrs. EM: <i>n</i>=62 tumor cells analyzed in 3 mice. SM: <i>n</i>=29 tumor cells analyzed in 3 mice. Log-rank (Mantel-Cox) test (<i>p</i>&lt;0.0001). ****<i>p</i>&lt;0.0001. <b>d</b> Percentage of E0771-GFP EM and SM tumor cells observed under the WHRIL that extravasated between 0 and 64 hrs after arrival. EM: <i>n</i>=89 tumor cells in 4 mice. SM: <i>n</i>=29 tumor cells in 3 mice. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. Unpaired two-tailed <i>t</i>-test (<i>p</i>=0.017). *<i>p</i>&lt;0.05. <b>e</b> Quantification of the time from arrival under the WHRIL to extravasation into the lung parenchyma for each E0771-GFP EM and SM tumor cell. <b>Left:</b> EM: <i>n</i>=11 tumor cells in 3 mice. <b>Right:</b> SM: <i>n</i>=21 tumor cells in 3 mice. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 1" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Similar to observations made with vacuum-based imaging windows for the lung<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Entenberg, D. et al. In vivo subcellular resolution optical imaging in the lung reveals early metastatic proliferation and motility. Intravital 4, 111 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1004">13</a></sup>, we observed that tumor cells arriving to the lung vasculature completely fill the capillary lumen and exclude all blood serum (as indicated by IV-injected fluorescent dextran), suggesting that these cells are arrested due to physical restraint rather than active attachment. We did not find that tumor cells roll along the vasculature or otherwise migrate from their place of lodgment, as was previously observed in lung explants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Al-Mehdi, A. B. et al. Intravascular origin of metastasis from the proliferation of endothelium-attached tumor cells: a new model for metastasis. Nat. Med. 6, 100102 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1008">8</a></sup> or in zebrafish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Follain, G. et al. Hemodynamic Forces Tune the Arrest, Adhesion, and Extravasation of Circulating Tumor Cells. Dev. Cell 45, 3352 e12 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1012">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Osmani, N. et al. Metastatic Tumor Cells Exploit Their Adhesion Repertoire to Counteract Shear Forces during Intravascular Arrest. Cell Rep. 28, 2491500 e5 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1015">28</a></sup>. We never observed tumor cells to proliferate intravascularly, as reported by Al-Mehdi et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Al-Mehdi, A. B. et al. Intravascular origin of metastasis from the proliferation of endothelium-attached tumor cells: a new model for metastasis. Nat. Med. 6, 100102 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1019">8</a></sup>. We also never observed CTC clusters traversing capillaries as was observed in vitro by Au et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Au, S. H. et al. Clusters of circulating tumor cells traverse capillary-sized vessels. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 113, 49474952 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1023">29</a></sup>.</p><p>Since the progression of tumor cells through the metastatic cascade takes much longer than 8 hrs, we posited that the fate of disseminated tumor cells could be followed by imaging the vasculature once every 8 h, using in vivo microcartography<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Entenberg, D. et al. A permanent window for the murine lung enables high-resolution imaging of cancer metastasis. Nat. Methods 15, 7380 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1030">24</a></sup> to relocate the same cells and microvasculature during each imaging session. To rule out the possibility that tumor cells may migrate in or out of the field of view between imaging sessions, we analyzed our time-lapsed images to assess the motility of spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells before and after extravasation. As can be seen in Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">1</a>c Left &amp; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">1d Left</a>, we never observed tumor cells migrating beyond a single field of view. In fact, we were surprised to find that tumor cells were largely immobile. Based on our measurements, we projected that the fastest cells would take, at a minimum, 69 h to move from the center of the field of view to its boundary (256m). Thus, we next undertook evaluations of the long-term fate of disseminated tumor cells by capturing a single image of the vasculature once every eight hours.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells are retained in the lung significantly longer and in higher numbers compared to intravenously injected tumor cells</h3><p>To track the fate of each newly arriving DTC as it progresses through the metastatic cascade, we prepared mice using the same procedure described above and then directly visualized the lung vasculature to record the presence of any previously disseminated tumor cells (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1a</a>, Time Point Pre), or the empty vasculature (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1b</a>, Before Arrival). The location of each preexisting tumor cell (including pre-existing metastases) was recorded and these locations excluded from further analysis, as their precise arrival time was unknown. After 8h, we again imaged the vasculature using the WHRIL and recorded the locations of all newly arrived DTCs. These images constitute <i>t</i>=0 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1a</a>, Time Point 0 hrs) with cells arriving sometime between 0 and 8 h after the start of the experiment.</p><p>We then imaged the lung vasculature every 8 h thereafter (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1a</a>, Time Points 8 through 64 h), using microcartography to predict to the same location for each time point. This location was verified by visual inspection of the unique architecture of the microvasculature. The fate of each tumor cell was determined as described in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1b</a>, which depicts the lung vasculature before the arrival of a tumor cell (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1b</a>, Before Arrival, top and bottom), the lodged tumor cell upon arrival (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1b</a>, Arrival, top and bottom), and finally the fate of the tumor cell as either death or recirculation within the vasculature (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1b</a>, Fate, top), or extravasation (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1b</a>, Fate, bottom).</p><p>With this methodology, we determined the arrival and subsequent disappearance or extravasation of tumor cells within 8 h timeframes. Unexpectedly, we found that, after an initial modest decline, most E0771-GFP tumor cells (~70%) were retained in the lung for the entire experimental period (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1c</a>, blue curve). Though, we observed a greater initial decline in the 231-GFP model, the number of tumor cells retained in the lung also persisted in this model, so that ~35% remained after 64 h (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2a</a>, blue curve). This was in stark contrast to previous reports where it was observed that DTCs die off and are rapidly cleared from the tissue<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Fidler, I. J. Metastasis: quantitative analysis of distribution and fate of tumor emboli labeled with 125 I-5-iodo-2-deoxyuridine. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 45, 773782 (1970)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1091">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Hofer, K. G., Prensky, W. &amp; Hughes, W. L. Death and metastatic distribution of tumor cells in mice monitored with 125I-iododeoxy-uridine. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 43, 763773 (1969)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1094">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liotta, L. A., Vembu, D., Saini, R. K. &amp; Boone, C. In vivo monitoring of the death rate of artificial murine pulmonary micrometastases. Cancer Res38, 12311236 (1978)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1097">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mayhew, E. &amp; Glaves, D. Quantitation of tumorigenic disseminating and arrested cancer cells. Br. J. Cancer 50, 159166 (1984)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1097_1">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Basse, P., Hokland, P., Heron, I. &amp; Hokland, M. Fate of tumor cells injected into left ventricle of heart in BALB/c mice: role of natural killer cells. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 80, 657665 (1988)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1100">32</a></sup>. To eliminate the use of the WHRIL as a contributing variable, we sought to repeat these experiments with the same experimental metastasis model used in the prior publications.</p><p>After repeating our time-lapse imaging validation experiments to ensure that EM cells cannot migrate out of a field of view within 8h (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">1</a>c Right &amp; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">1d Right</a>), we established a comparable method for tracking DTCs within the experimental metastasis model. We implanted the WHRIL into mice and then 24 h later, we injected the same tumor cells into their tail veins (E0771-GFP in syngeneic C57/B6 or 231-GFP in nude mice). We then immediately began imaging the vasculature under the WHRIL. These images thus constitute <i>t</i>=0 for the EM model (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1a</a>, bottom, Time Point 0 h). We found that, as we had initially expected, E0771-GFP EM tumor cells were rapidly cleared from the lung, with a greater than 50% drop within the first 16 h, and a steady decline thereafter, leaving only 5% after 64 h (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1c</a>, red curve). This represents a 10-fold increase in tumor cell retention of SM compared to EM tumor cells.</p><p>Consistently, we observed that the number of 231-GFP EM tumor cells also rapidly declined to ~40% within the first 16 h, and then continued to decline steadily until less than 2% remained at 64 h postinjection (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2a</a>, red curve). Although the difference between EM and SM in this model was not as large as in the syngeneic model, the difference between the two models persisted and remained significant, confirming that disseminating tumor cells that originate in a primary tumor are retained longer, and in higher numbers, within the secondary site of the lung.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells extravasate more quickly than intravenously injected tumor cells</h3><p>It has been hypothesized that extravasation is a major rate-limiting step in metastasis because tumor cells are killed by the mechanical trauma that they are subjected to in the circulatory system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Weiss, L., Nannmark, U., Johansson, B. R. &amp; Bagge, U. Lethal deformation of cancer cells in the microcirculation: a potential rate regulator of hematogenous metastasis. Int. J. Cancer 50, 103107 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1137">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Huang, Q. et al. Fluid shear stress and tumor metastasis. Am. J. Cancer Res. 8, 763777 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1140">34</a></sup>, and when tumor cells are able to extravasate quickly, they metastasize more efficiently<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Spiegel, A. et al. Neutrophils Suppress Intraluminal NK Cell-Mediated Tumor Cell Clearance and Enhance Extravasation of Disseminated Carcinoma Cells. Cancer Discov. 6, 630649 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1144">5</a></sup>. We therefore posited that the increased rate of retention of SM cells is due to a better ability to extravasate. To test this, we used the WHRIL to analyze the number of tumor cells that extravasated within the experimental time period (between 0 and 64 h). While only 16% of EM cells were able to extravasate, a significantly greater proportion of SM cells (64%) was able to extravasate during the same time period after arrival to the lung (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1d</a>). Interestingly, while we did observe a slight increase in the ability of SM cells to extravasate in the 231-GFP model, this was not statistically different from EM cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2b</a>).</p><p>The observation that syngeneic SM cells extravasate more efficiently prompted us to evaluate how long individual tumor cells arriving in the lung take to cross the vascular endothelium. We therefore imaged SM and EM cells every 8 h after their arrival under the WHRIL and observed no EM cells to extravasate between 0 and 8 h after intravenous (iv) injection. The distribution of extravasation times was wide with many cells taking as long as 4048 h to cross the endothelium (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1e</a>, red bars) with the average time of extravasation being 28.04.1 h. In contrast, we found that a much larger proportion of SM cells was able to cross the endothelium quickly, with ~50% doing so within 08h from the time of the first arrival to the lung vasculature (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1e</a>, blue bars). By 24 h, the vast majority of SM tumor cells had extravasated. Moreover, the average time of extravasation for SM cells was nearly half that for EM cells (11.61.9 hrs, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1e</a>). Similar observations were made with 231-GFP cells, where only ~30% of EM tumor cells extravasated within the 08 h period while ~80% of the SM cells extravasated in the same period (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2c</a>). While the distribution of extravasation times was not as wide for the EM 231-GFP cells as was observed for the EM E0771-GFP cells, the difference in means persisted with EM cells extravasating on average at 11.81.0 h vs. 6.40.8h for SM cells. These data demonstrate that disseminating tumor cells that originate in a primary tumor extravasate much more quickly than intravenously injected tumor cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Expression of Mena<sup>INV</sup> confers early extravasation competency to disseminated tumor cells</h3><p>Our previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Macrophage-dependent tumor cell transendothelial migration is mediated by Notch1/MenaINV-initiated invadopodium formation. Sci. Rep. 6, 37874 (2016)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1180">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roussos, E. T. et al. Mena invasive (MenaINV) promotes multicellular streaming motility and transendothelial migration in a mouse model of breast cancer. J. Cell Sci. 124, 21202131 (2011)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1180_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Philippar, U. et al. A Mena invasion isoform potentiates EGF-induced carcinoma cell invasion and metastasis. Dev. Cell 15, 813828 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1183">37</a></sup> showed that expression of alternative splice variants of the actin regulatory protein, Mena, confer dramatically different metastatic phenotypes to tumor cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Gertler, F. &amp; Condeelis, J. Metastasis: tumor cells becoming MENAcing. Trends Cell Biol. 21, 8190 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1187">38</a></sup>. Expression of the isoform, Mena11a, is associated with an epithelial, non-metastatic phenotype. Meanwhile Mena<sup>INV</sup>, an isoform not expressed in tumor cells cultured in vitro, but induced upon contact with macrophages, enhances tumor cell migration and transendothelial migration during intravasation within the primary tumor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Macrophage-dependent tumor cell transendothelial migration is mediated by Notch1/MenaINV-initiated invadopodium formation. Sci. Rep. 6, 37874 (2016)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1193">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Roussos, E. T. et al. Mena invasive (MenaINV) promotes multicellular streaming motility and transendothelial migration in a mouse model of breast cancer. J. Cell Sci. 124, 21202131 (2011)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1193_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Philippar, U. et al. A Mena invasion isoform potentiates EGF-induced carcinoma cell invasion and metastasis. Dev. Cell 15, 813828 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1196">37</a></sup>.</p><p>Therefore, we hypothesized that Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression could play a similar role during extravasation at the secondary site by conferring to tumor cells the ability to cross the endothelium quickly, a role never before explored for this protein. As a preliminary test, we took tissue sections from the lungs of the EM and SM models and stained them for GFP (to identify tumor cells), Mena<sup>INV</sup>, endomucin (to identify the vasculature), and DAPI (to identify all nuclei) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig2">2a</a>). We found a ~25-fold increase (49% vs. 2%) in the percentage of SM tumor cells expressing Mena<sup>INV</sup>, compared to EM cells (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig2">2b</a>). In the 231-GFP model, the percent of cells expressing Mena<sup>INV</sup> was ~4 times higher <i>(</i>48% vs. 13%) for SM compared to EM cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2d, e</a>). This shows that Mena isoform expression in tumor cells retained in the lungs is significantly different between SM and EM cells.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="MenaINV expression in disseminated tumor cells regulates extravasation efficiency."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression in disseminated tumor cells regulates extravasation efficiency.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig2" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure 2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="401"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a Left:</b> Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression in extravascular E0771-GFP tumor cells in the lung of an EM model (top) and an SM model (bottom). Green arrow: Mena<sup>INV</sup> negative tumor cell. Red arrows: Mena<sup>INV</sup> positive tumor cells. Scale bar=50 m. <b>Right:</b> Zoomed in view of a disseminated tumor cell (yellow box) in both models. Green=GFP, Red=Mena<sup>INV</sup>, White=endomucin, Blue=DAPI. Scale bar=10m. <b>b</b> Quantification of extravascular Mena<sup>INV</sup> positive disseminated tumor cells in the lung of each group from Fig. 2a. EM: <i>n</i>=41 cells in 5 animals. SM: <i>n</i>=89 cells in 7 animals. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. Two-tailed Mann-Whitney test (<i>p</i>=0.0025). **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01. <b>c</b> Western blot of 231-GFP-Mena11a and 231-GFP-Mena<sup>INV</sup> cells. -Actin was used as a loading control. Full blot is presented in Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">16</a>. <b>d</b> Outline of experimental design to determine percentage of tumor cells able to extravasate between 0 and 8 h after iv-injection. Intravital imaging of the lung vasculature through the WHRIL was performed before (Timepoint=Pre), immediately after (Time point=0 h), and then finally 8 h after tumor cell injection (Time point=8 h). <b>e</b> Percentage of tumor cells that extravasated between 0 and 8 h after iv injection. 231-GFP-Mena11a: <i>n</i>=90 cells in 3 mice. 231-GFP-Mena<sup>INV</sup>: <i>n</i>=88 tumor cells in 3 mice. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. Two-tailed unpaired <i>t</i>-test (<i>p</i>=0.0048). **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 2" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine whether the expression of Mena<sup>INV</sup> directly confers the ability to extravasate early (within 8hr of arrival in the lung vasculature), we performed gain- and loss-of-function experiments using two genetically modified 231-GFP cell lines: one that overexpresses Mena<sup>INV</sup> (231-GFP-Mena<sup>INV</sup>) and one that overexpresses Mena11a but does not express Mena<sup>INV</sup> at all (231-GFP-Mena11a)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Roussos, E. T. et al. Mena invasive (Mena(INV)) and Mena11a isoforms play distinct roles in breast cancer cell cohesion and association with TMEM. Clin. Exp. Metastasis 28, 515527 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1322">39</a></sup> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig2">2c</a>). Using these two cell lines, we performed the experimental metastasis assay and tracked the number of tumor cells that had extravasated after 8 hr (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig2">2d</a>). Over 50% of the 231-GFP-Mena<sup>INV</sup> cells that arrived under the WHRIL extravasated within the first 8 hr post-injection, while lack of Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression almost completely abrogated tumor cell extravasation within this time period (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig2">2e</a>). This resulted in a nearly 30-fold increase in early extravasation of Mena<sup>INV</sup> (54%) compared to Mena 11a (2%) expressing cells. These data show that the selective expression of Mena<sup>INV</sup>, which is only upregulated in tumor cells that have successfully intravasated, greatly enhances the efficiency of early extravasation of DTCs into secondary sites.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells survive significantly longer at the secondary site compared to intravenously injected tumor cells</h3><p>It is generally accepted that, after extravasation into the lungs, only a very small percentage of tumor cells survives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wong, C. W. et al. Apoptosis: an early event in metastatic inefficiency. Cancer Res. 61, 333338 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1354">40</a></sup>. Given the differences between SM and EM observed thus far, we aimed to test whether the presence of the primary tumor influences tumor cell survival after extravasation in the lung. Thus, we imaged the lung vasculature in the SM and EM models, as described in Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1a</a>, every eight hours for 64 hrs to track each individual tumor cell longitudinally. There were three possible outcomes for disseminated tumor cells: they died (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig3">3a</a>, left), evidenced by the presence of GFP+cellular debris in the parenchyma, as was previously observed by Kienast et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kienast, Y. et al. Real-time imaging reveals the single steps of brain metastasis formation. Nat. Med. 16, 116122 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1364">12</a></sup> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig3">3a</a>, left, yellow arrow); they survived in the lung parenchyma as single cells (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig3">3a</a>, middle); or they grew into micro-metastases (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig3">3a</a>, right).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells survive significantly longer at the secondary site compared to intravenously injected tumor cells."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells survive significantly longer at the secondary site compared to intravenously injected tumor cells.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig3" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure 3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="779"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Representative intravital microscopy images showing the possible fates of extravascular disseminated tumor cells in the lung parenchyma. <b>Top:</b> Images of disseminated tumor cells just after extravasation. <b>Bottom left:</b> Example of an extravascular tumor cell, which has died, as evidenced by small extravascular apoptotic bodies (yellow arrow). <b>Bottom middle:</b> Example of an extravascular tumor cell that survived as a single and solitary tumor cell over time. <b>Bottom right:</b> Example of an extravascular tumor cell that began to divide and grow into a micro-metastasis. Red=tdTomato labeled endothelial cells and 155kDa Tetramethylrhodamine dextran labeled blood serum, Green=GFP labeled tumor cells. Yellow dashed lines delineate blood vessel boundaries. Scale bar=15m. <b>b</b> Percentage of extravascular E0771-GFP disseminated tumor cells that died, survived, or grew after extravasation in EM and SM models 64 hrs after arrival to the lung vasculature. EM: <i>n</i>=27 tumor cells in 4 mice. SM: <i>n</i>=31 tumor cells in 4 mice. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. For Died and Survived columns, a two-tailed unpaired t-test was used (<i>p</i>=0.0003 and 0.0005, respectively). For Grew columns, a two-tailed Mann-Whitney test was used (<i>p</i>=0.14). ***<i>p</i>&lt;0.001. ns=not significant. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 3" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We found that a large majority (72%) of EM cells died shortly after extravasation, with a 28% survival rate at 64 hrs. Of these, 10% remained as single cells at 64 h postinjection and 18% began to form micro-metastasis (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig3">3b</a> left, red bars). By 64 h after extravasation, the vast majority of SM cells (84%) survived as single and solitary tumor cells and only a small percentage (13%) died. Of the surviving cells, only a small subset formed micrometastasis (3%) during this time period (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig3">3b</a> left, blue bars). We find no statistical difference in the number of cells that eventually developed into micro-metastasis between the two models. Similar observations were made with 231-GFP cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2f</a>). These data demonstrate that disseminated tumor cells originating in a primary tumor have a drastically increased ability to extravasate and successfully survive as solitary cells in the lung parenchyma compared to intravenously injected tumor cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells have higher expression of dormancy markers than intravenously injected tumor cells</h3><p>The observation that the vast majority of SM cells survive as single and solitary cells, without dying or proliferating during the first 64 hrs of their residency in the lung, suggested that they may have become dormant. This is consistent with earlier studies using EM to disseminate tumor cells in the CAM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="MacDonald, I. C., Schmidt, E. E., Morris, V. L., Chambers, A. F. &amp; Groom, A. C. Intravital videomicroscopy of the chorioallantoic microcirculation: a model system for studying metastasis. Microvasc Res. 44, 185199 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1451">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Morris, V. L. et al. Mammary carcinoma cell lines of high and low metastatic potential differ not in extravasation but in subsequent migration and growth. Clin. Exp. Metastasis 12, 357367 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1454">26</a></sup>, liver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Naumov, G. N. et al. Persistence of solitary mammary carcinoma cells in a secondary site: a possible contributor to dormancy. Cancer Res. 62, 21622168 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1458">41</a></sup>, and lung<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Cameron, M. D. et al. Temporal progression of metastasis in lung: cell survival, dormancy, and location dependence of metastatic inefficiency. Cancer Res. 60, 25412546 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1462">17</a></sup>. However, in those studies, the dormant state of the tumor cells was only determined using tools that assess active proliferation (absence of division, Ki-67 expression). This prompted us to assess if the solitary DTCs we found in the SM model are also in a dormant state using more recently discovered markers. One of the best molecular markers of dormancy is the orphan nuclear receptor NR2F1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Borgen, E. et al. NR2F1 stratifies dormant disseminated tumor cells in breast cancer patients. Breast Cancer Res. 20, 120 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1466">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1469">43</a></sup>, a member of the retinoic acid receptor family<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1473">43</a></sup> that has been shown to be a marker for cancer dormancy in pre-clinical models and cancer patients, as well to be an independent prognostic indicator for time-to-distant-recurrence in breast cancer patients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Borgen, E. et al. NR2F1 stratifies dormant disseminated tumor cells in breast cancer patients. Breast Cancer Res. 20, 120 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1478">42</a></sup>. Furthermore, NR2F1 has been shown to regulate tumor dormancy in different mouse models, including breast cancer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS One 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1482">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1485">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1488">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gao, X. L. et al. NR2F1 contributes to cancer cell dormancy, invasion and metastasis of salivary adenoid cystic carcinoma by activating CXCL12/CXCR4 pathway. BMC Cancer 19, 743 (2019)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1488_1">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Khalil, B. D. et al. An NR2F1 specific agonist suppresses metastasis by inducing cancer cell dormancy. J. Exp. Med. 2021 (In Press)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1491">45</a></sup>.</p><p>In tissue and CTCs from both metastasis models, we could find single tumor cells (GFP+) that expressed nuclear NR2F1 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4a</a>). We found that in lung tissues, the frequency of disseminated tumor cells expressing nuclear NR2F1 was upregulated ~3-fold in SM cells when compared to EM cells (53% vs. 19%) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4b</a>, Lung columns), suggesting that a significantly greater proportion of spontaneously disseminated tumor cells enter dormancy as supported by prior studies using SM protocols<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1504">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Khalil, B. D. et al. An NR2F1 specific agonist suppresses metastasis by inducing cancer cell dormancy. J. Exp. Med. 2021 (In Press)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1507">45</a></sup>. We also observed numerous instances of NR2F1-positive tumor cells located inside the lung vasculature (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">3</a>). This raised the question of whether SM tumor cell dormancy was initiated after arrival to the lung vasculature, or if it occurred within the primary tumor, as we previously showed happens under hypoxic conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1514">21</a></sup>. We therefore quantified NRF21+cells in primary tumor tissues and circulating tumor cells (CTCs) from the SM model (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4a</a>, Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">4a</a>). Only a very small percentage (~3%) of primary tumor cells were positive for NR2F1 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4b</a>, Primary Tumor bar), consistent with data from head and neck squamous cell carcinoma (HNSCC) PDX models and human HNSCC tumors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1528">21</a></sup> and the mouse mammary tumor virus-polyoma virus middle T antigen (MMTV-PyMT) model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1532">21</a></sup>. Despite there being only a small number of NR2F1-positive primary tumor cells, we observed that ~50% of CTCs were NR2F1-positive (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4b</a>, CTCs bar), suggesting that tumor cells are programmed for dormancy either before they intravasate (since acquisition can occur in hypoxic microenvironments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1540">21</a></sup>) or during intravasation. This represents a very significant enrichment (~17-fold) upon intravasation. Given the short residence time of the cells in the vasculature as CTCs, it is unlikely this enrichment is due to the death of non-dormant cells within the systemic circulation. This high level of NR2F1 DTC positivity was also found in disseminated tumor cells in the lung tissue (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4b</a>, left Lung bar). Importantly, we did not observe a difference between the percentage of NR2F1-positive cells present in vitro, before injection, and the fraction of EM cells observed in the lung three days postinjection (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4b</a>, right, in vitro &amp; Lung bars), indicating that expression of NR2F1 is not influenced by passage through the blood. Similar observations were made with 231-GFP cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">4b</a>), though the percentage of NR2F1 cells within the SM lung was significantly lower than in the CTCs. Overall, these data show that a larger proportion of tumor cells that originate in a primary tumor and arrive to the lungs express dormancy markers compared to intravenously injected tumor cells.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells are more frequently positive for dormancy or stem-like markers compared to intravenously injected tumor cells."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells are more frequently positive for dormancy or stem-like markers compared to intravenously injected tumor cells.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig4" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure 4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="550"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Representative immunofluorescence images of NR2F1 expression in primary tumors, circulating tumor cells (CTCs), and disseminated tumor cells (Lung) from an E0771-GFP SM model (Left) and in disseminated tumor cells (Lung) from an EM model (right). Green=GFP, Red=NR2F1, Blue=DAPI. Scale bar for Primary Tumor=50m. Scale bar for CTCs and Lung=15m. <b>b</b> Percentage of NR2F1-positive and negative tumor cells in each group in <b>A</b>. Primary Tumor: <i>n</i>=2,041 cells in 115 fields of view (6565m<sup>2</sup>) in 8 animals; CTCs: <i>n</i>=550 cells in 8 animals; SM Lung: <i>n</i>=237 cells in 12 animals; EM Lung: <i>n</i>=199 cells in 8 animals. In vitro: <i>n</i>=463 cells in 5 independent experiments. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. For all comparisons, a two-tailed one-way ANOVA test with Sidaks multiple comparisons adjustment was used (PT vs. CTC: <i>p</i>&lt;0.0001; PT vs. Lung SM: <i>p</i>&lt;0.0001; CTC vs. Lung SM: <i>p</i>=0.80; Lung SM vs. Lung EM: <i>p</i>&lt;0.0001; In vitro vs. Lung EM: <i>p</i>=1.00). ****<i>p</i>&lt;0.0001. ns=not significant. <b>c</b> Representative immunofluorescence images of SOX9 expression in primary tumors, circulating tumor cells (CTCs), and disseminated tumor cells (Lung) from an E0771-GFP SM model (Left) and in disseminated tumor cells (Lung) from an EM model (right). Green=GFP, Red=SOX9, Blue=DAPI. Scale bar for Primary Tumor=50m. Scale bar for CTCs and Lung=15 m. <b>d</b> Percentage of SOX9<sup>High</sup> tumor cells from each group in <b>C</b>.
