Primary Tumor: <i>n</i>=4,633 cells in 150 fields of view (6565 m<sup>2</sup>) in 8 animals; CTCs: <i>n</i>=558 cells in 5 animals, SM Lung: <i>n</i>=341 cells in 11 animals; EM Lung: <i>n</i>=182 cells in 8 animals. In vitro: <i>n</i>=298 cells in 3 independent experiments. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. For CTC vs. Lung SM (<i>p</i>=0.89) a two-tailed one-way ANOVA test with Sidaks multiple comparisons adjustment was used. For PT <i>vs</i>. CTC: (<i>p</i>=0.0027), PT vs. Lung SM (<i>p</i>=0.0002), In vitro vs. Lung EM (<i>p</i>=1.00), and Lung SM vs. Lung EM (<i>p</i>=0.0011) a two-tailed Kruskal-Wallis test with Dunns multiple comparisons adjustment was used. **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01. ***<i>p</i>&lt;0.001. ns=not significant. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 4" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells are more frequently positive for both dormancy and stem-like markers compared to intravenously injected tumor cells</h3><p>Recently, it was shown<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1681">43</a></sup> that NR2F1 in tumor cells coordinates the expression of other genes that are found in self-renewing embryonic stem cells (e.g. SOX9, SOX2, and NANOG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Hadjimichael, C. et al. Common stemness regulators of embryonic and cancer stem cells. World J. Stem Cells 7, 11501184 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1685">46</a></sup>) and that can themselves coordinate NR2F1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Rada-Iglesias, A. et al. Epigenomic annotation of enhancers predicts transcriptional regulators of human neural crest. Cell Stem Cell 11, 633648 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1689">47</a></sup>. In particular, it was discovered that NR2F1 binds directly to the promoter of SOX9 to regulate SOX9 expression in tumor cells, resulting in dormancy and growth arrest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1693">43</a></sup>. Furthermore, it was observed that SOX2 mRNA is significantly upregulated in dormant tumor cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1697">43</a></sup>. Based on these observations, we hypothesized that tumor cells originating in a primary tumor may take on a more stem-like and growth arresting phenotype compared to intravenously injected tumor cells as part of the dormancy program that is induced in the primary tumor.</p><p>To address this, lung tissue sections from EM and SM models, primary tumor tissues, and CTCs from the SM model, were stained for SOX9 (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4c</a>). The expression of SOX9 in SM cells in the lung was ~4-fold higher when compared to EM cells (41% vs. 11%, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4d</a>). As with NR2F1, we found only a small population (4%) of SOX9<sup>High</sup> cells in the primary tumor (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4d</a>, Primary Tumor bar), but a dramatic enrichment of SOX9<sup>High</sup> CTCs (39%, a ~10-fold increase, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4d</a>, CTCs bar), suggesting that tumor cells are programmed for stemness in the primary tumor, before, or during intravasation. Again, we did not observe a difference between the percentage of SOX9<sup>High</sup> cells in vitro and the fraction of EM cells in the lung (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig4">4b</a>, right, in vitro &amp; Lung bars), suggesting that induction of a stem-like program does not occur in the blood. Similar observations were made with 231-GFP cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">4c, d</a>).</p><p>Given the mechanistic link between NR2F1 and SOX9 expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1732">43</a></sup>, we determined whether disseminated tumor cells co-express NR2F1 and SOX9, which would result in both quiescence and self-renewal (a stem-like program, such as exists in adult quiescent stem cells). Lung tissue from EM and SM models, primary tumor tissues, and CTCs from the SM model were stained for GFP, NR2F1, SOX9, and DAPI (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig5">5a</a> &amp; Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">4e</a>). In the primary tumor, we observed only a small population (4%) that co-expressed NR2F1 and SOX9. However, SM cells were enriched for double-positive CTCs and DTCs in the lung compared EM cells (37% vs. 1% for each, Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig5">5b</a>). Similar observations were made with 231-GFP cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">4f</a>). Overall, these data show that SM tumor cells become progressively more enriched in dormancy and stem-like phenotypes (as evidenced by expression of markers of dormancy and stemness) as they disseminate from the primary tumor to the lung.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells are more frequently doubly positive for dormancy and stem-like markers compared to intravenously injected tumor cells."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Spontaneously metastasizing tumor cells are more frequently doubly positive for dormancy and stem-like markers compared to intravenously injected tumor cells.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig5" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure 5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="318"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b> Representative images of triple immunofluorescence staining for GFP, NR2F1, and SOX9 expression in primary tumors, circulating tumor cells (CTCs), and disseminated tumor cells (Lung) from an E0771-GFP SM model (Left) and in disseminated tumor cells (Lung) from an EM model (Right). Green=GFP; Red=NR2F1; Orange=SOX9; Blue=DAPI. Scale bar for Primary Tumor=50m. Scale bar for CTCs and Lung=15m. <b>b</b> Percentage of double-positive tumor cells NR2F1-positive SOX9<sup>High</sup> from each group in Fig. 5a. Primary Tumor: <i>n</i>=2383 in 97 fields of view (6565m<sup>2</sup>) in 7 animals; CTCs: <i>n</i>=379 cells in 8 animals; SM Lung: <i>n</i>=104 cells in 9 animals; In vitro: <i>n</i>=413 cells in 3 independent experiments. EM Lung: <i>n</i>=75 cells in 7 animals. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. For EM Lung vs. SM Lung (<i>p</i>=0.0001) and EM Lung vs. in vitro (<i>p</i>=0.69), a two-tailed Kruskal-Wallis test with Dunns multiple comparisons adjustment was used. For PT <i>vs</i>. CTC: (<i>p</i>=0.0041), PT vs. Lung SM (<i>p</i>=0.0030), and CTC <i>vs</i>. Lung SM (<i>p</i>=1.00) a two-tailed ANOVA test with Sidaks multiple comparisons adjustment was used. **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01. ***<i>p</i>&lt;0.001. ns=not significant. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 5" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">NR2F1 regulates the fate of extravasated tumor cells</h3><p>These data raise the question of what impact NR2F1 expression has on the ability of DTCs to metastasize. We previously demonstrated that NR2F1 in tumor cells does impact metastasis, with its downregulation increasing metastasis in the lung and spleen, as well as in surgery margins where dormant residual cells persist<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1830">43</a></sup>, and its activation suppressing metastatic growth post-extravasation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Khalil, B. D. et al. An NR2F1 specific agonist suppresses metastasis by inducing cancer cell dormancy. J. Exp. Med. 2021 (In Press)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1834">45</a></sup>. However, it is still unknown what impact this downregulation has on the ability of tumor cells to extravasate, or whether cells with downregulated NR2F1 are immediately able to begin proliferating in the lung.</p><p>To address the question of what impact reduction of NR2F1 expression has on the ability of tumor cells to extravasate, we downregulated NR2F1 in tumor cells using the Tet-ON-inducible shRNAmir-NR2F1 (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">5ac</a>), as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1844">43</a></sup>. We then tail vein injected these cells and measured their kinetics of extravasation using intravital imaging, tracking the number of tumor cells that extravasated between 0 and 24 hrs (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">5d</a> top). We found that, whiledownregulation of NR2F1 limits the extravasation ability of tumor cells by ~10%, still ~25% of the DTCs with knockdown are able to extravasate, suggesting a minor role for NR2F1 on this step (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">5e</a>).</p><p>To determine whether reduction of NR2F1 expression makes DTCs begin to proliferate immediately, we tail vein injected shNR2F1 DTCs into mice both with and without doxycycline (to regulate Tet-ON-inducible shRNAmir-NR2F1) and then stained their lungs for GFP to identify micro-metastatic foci at 4 and 8 days after injection. We found that neither knockdown nor NR2F1-expressing DTCs begin proliferating immediately, but knockdown cells are significantly more proliferative by 8 days postinjection (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">5f</a>). We additionally confirmed these findings in a spontaneous metastasis model (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">5d</a>, bottom &amp; Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">5g</a>).</p><p>Overall, these data demonstrate that NR2F1 does determine the fate of DTCs, and its regulation affects metastatic growth primarily by regulating a post-extravasation dormant state combined with a less significant contribution to extravasation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">NR2F1 and Mena<sup>INV</sup> positive tumor cells are preferentially associated with TMEM doorways in the primary tumor</h3><p>Given the dramatic increase in dormancy markers as tumor cells move from the primary tumor into the vasculature, we hypothesized that the dormancy program may be activated near to, or at, intravasation sites. Our prior work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Harney, A. S. et al. Real-Time Imaging Reveals Local, Transient Vascular Permeability, and Tumor Cell Intravasation Stimulated by TIE2hi Macrophage-Derived VEGFA. Cancer Dis. 5, 932943 (2015)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1880">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wyckoff, J. B. et al. Direct visualization of macrophage-assisted tumor cell intravasation in mammary tumors. Cancer Res. 67, 26492656 (2007)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1880_1">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Robinson, B. D. et al. Tumor microenvironment of metastasis in human breast carcinoma: a potential prognostic marker linked to hematogenous dissemination. Clin. Cancer Res. 15, 24332441 (2009)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1880_2">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rohan, T. E. et al. Tumor microenvironment of metastasis and risk of distant metastasis of breast cancer. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 106, dju136 (2014)." href="#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1880_3">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Sparano, J. A. et al. A metastasis biomarker (MetaSite Breast Score) is associated with distant recurrence in hormone receptor-positive, HER2-negative early-stage breast cancer. NPJ Breast Cancer 3, 42 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1883">52</a></sup> has shown that tumor cells intravasate through cellular doorways in the vasculature called tumor microenvironment of metastasis (TMEM) doorways. This stable triple cell complex is composed of a Mena<sup>High</sup> tumor cell, a Tie2<sup>High</sup> macrophage, and a blood vessel endothelial cell, all in direct physical contact. We have shown that TMEM doorways are the sole sites of breast cancer cell intravasation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Oktay, M. H. &amp; Jones, J. G. TMEM: a novel breast cancer dissemination marker for the assessment of metastatic risk. Biomark Med. 9, 8184 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1891">53</a></sup> and we have clinically validated the density of TMEM doorways as a prognostic marker for metastatic recurrence in breast cancer patients<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Sparano, J. A. et al. A metastasis biomarker (MetaSite Breast Score) is associated with distant recurrence in hormone receptor-positive, HER2-negative early-stage breast cancer. NPJ Breast Cancer 3, 42 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1895">52</a></sup>. Thus, programming for dormancy might be induced as migratory tumor cells approach, interact with, or reside in the vicinity of TMEM doorways.</p><p>Serial sections of primary breast tumor tissues were stained for TMEM doorways using a triple immunohistochemical stain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Robinson, B. D. et al. Tumor microenvironment of metastasis in human breast carcinoma: a potential prognostic marker linked to hematogenous dissemination. Clin. Cancer Res. 15, 24332441 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1902">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Sparano, J. A. et al. A metastasis biomarker (MetaSite Breast Score) is associated with distant recurrence in hormone receptor-positive, HER2-negative early-stage breast cancer. NPJ Breast Cancer 3, 42 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e1905">52</a></sup> (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig6">6a</a>, left and insets) or with GFP to identify tumor cells, NR2F1, and DAPI (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig6">6a</a>, right and rightmost inset). Using digital whole slide scanning and tissue alignment software (see Methods), we were able to co-register the two slides down to the single-cell level and measure the relative distance from each NR2F1-positive tumor cell to the nearest TMEM doorway. Analysis of these distances revealed an ~2.2-fold enrichment of NR2F1-positive tumor cells near TMEM doorways (080m), compared to tumor cells farther away (160-200m) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig6">6b</a>, red curve). Interestingly, NR2F1-positive tumor cell enrichment was specifically associated with TMEM doorways, as we did not find any enrichment around blood vessels lacking TMEM doorways (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig6">6c</a>, red curve). These data indicate that tumor cells within the primary tumor, and in close proximity to TMEM doorways, upregulate the expression of the dormancy marker NR2F1.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Dormant tumor cells are preferentially associated with TMEM doorways in the primary tumor."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6: Dormant tumor cells are preferentially associated with TMEM doorways in the primary tumor.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig6" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure 6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="762"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p><b>a Left:</b> Representative image of triple immunohistochemical stain in E0771-GFP primary tumor for the cells composing TMEM doorway positioned at vertices of yellow triangle: Mena expressing tumor cells=pink; IBA-1 expressing macrophages = brown; endomucin expressing endothelial cells=blue. Red dashed circle encompasses the perimeter of TMEM doorway. Scale bar=60m. Insets are zoom-in of boxed region (first panel on left). Other insets show color deconvolutions for each of the stains (Mena, Endomucin, IBA-1). TTC=TMEM Doorway Tumor Cell. EC=Endothelial Cell. M=Macrophage. Scale bar=15m. <b>Right</b> Sequential slide of tissue in Left panel, immunofluorescently stained for NR2F1 expressing tumor cells: GFP=green; NR2F1=red; DAPI=blue. Red dashed circle encompasses the perimeter of TMEM doorway. Vertices of the orange triangles point to each constitutive cell. Red arrow points to NR2F1-positive cells. <b>b</b> Quantification showing the frequency of distances between NR2F1 positive tumor cells (red dots) or NR2F1 and Mena<sup>INV</sup> double-positive tumor cells (blue dots) to the nearest TMEM doorway in the primary tumor. Data is normalized to the frequency of distances between all DAPI positive nuclei to the nearest TMEM doorway. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. <i>n</i>=10 13 mm<sup>2</sup> regions of interest area in four mice for NR2F1, and <i>n</i>=eight 13 mm<sup>2</sup> regions of interest area in 4 mice for NR2F1 and Mena<sup>INV</sup>. For comparison between the 0 and 200m bins, a two-tailed unpaired t-test was used (Mena<sup>INV</sup>+: <i>p</i>=0.0004; NR2F1+Mena<sup>INV</sup>+: <i>p</i>=0.029). *<i>p</i>&lt;0.05. ***<i>p</i>&lt;0.001. <b>c</b> Quantification showing the frequency of distances between NR2F1<sup>+</sup> tumor cells or NR2F1<sup>+</sup> (red dots) and Mena<sup>INV+</sup> tumor cells (blue dots) to the nearest blood vessel lacking TMEM in the primary tumor. Data is normalized to the frequency of distances between all DAPI positive nuclei to the nearest blood vessel lacking TMEM. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. <i>n</i>=nine 13mm<sup>2</sup> regions of interest area in four mice for NR2F1, and <i>n</i>=eight 13mm<sup>2</sup> regions of interest area in fourmice for NR2F1 and Mena<sup>INV</sup>. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 6" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Given the high level of enrichment of NR2F1 positive CTCs (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig5">5b</a>), and that Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression is upregulated near TMEM doorways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Sharma, V. P. et al. Live tumor imaging shows macrophage induction and TMEM-mediated enrichment of cancer stem cells during metastatic dissemination. Nat. Commun. (In Press)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2012">54</a></sup>, we asked whether the two proteins are co-expressed within the same cells. This is expected as intravasation is dependent upon Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Macrophage-dependent tumor cell transendothelial migration is mediated by Notch1/MenaINV-initiated invadopodium formation. Sci. Rep. 6, 37874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2018">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Gligorijevic, B. et al. N-WASP-mediated invadopodium formation is involved in intravasation and lung metastasis of mammary tumors. J. Cell Sci. 125, 724734 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2021">55</a></sup>. Thus, we stained primary breast tumors for GFP, Mena<sup>INV</sup>, NR2F1, and TMEM doorways (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">6</a>), and performed a similar distance analysis to that described above.</p><p>As expected, our analysis revealed an enrichment (~3.2-fold) of double Mena<sup>INV</sup>-positive and NR2F1-positive tumor cells at TMEM doorways (080m), compared to tumor cells farther away (160200m) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig6">6b</a>, blue curve). No enrichment was observed relative to blood vessels without TMEM doorways (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig6">6c</a>, blue curve).</p><p>Taken together, these data demonstrate that tumor cells that are destined to disseminate, become upregulated in their expression of markers of dormancy as they approach intravasation sites.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">DTCs are upregulated in their expression of proteins associated with dormancy and dissemination, and this expression is lost with metastatic growth in the lung</h3><p>Given that tumor cells acquire pro-dissemination and dormancy phenotypes as they approach TMEM doorways (where they can intravasate and disseminate), we asked next whether these phenotypes are maintained as they arrive to and take residence in the secondary site of the lung. To this end, we took lung tissues from the SM model, stained them for NR2F1 and Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">7a</a>), and quantified the percent of single and double-positive tumor cells in single DTCs and in small (10 tumor cells), medium (11300 tumor cells), and large (300 tumor cells) micro-metastases (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">7b</a>). We found that DTCs are equally likely (~25% each) to express any combination of the two markers. However, as the tumor cells begin to grow, this expression pattern is progressively lost with the number of double negative TCs growing to ~90% of the large metastases and double-positive cells accounting for ~1%. These data are consistent with our previous observation that NR2F1 expression is lost in growing metastatic lesions in both mouse models and human metastasis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2062">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M. S. et al. NR2F1 controls tumour cell dormancy via SOX9- and RARbeta-driven quiescence programmes. Nat. Commun. 6, 6170 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2065">43</a></sup> and additionally demonstrates that Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression is also lost. Altogether, these data show that tumor cells destined to disseminate, acquire a pro-dissemination and dormancy phenotype in the primary tumor that is carried to the secondary site, and is lost during metastatic growth.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Macrophages regulate the expression of NR2F1 in tumor cells</h3><p>It is currently unknown whether macrophages in general, and whether macrophage-tumor cell interactions around TMEM doorways more specifically, are able to regulate NR2F1 expression on cancer cells. In order to investigate this, we studied the spatial distribution of NR2F1-positive tumor cells relative to macrophages in fixed primary breast tumors (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig7">7a</a>). We observed an ~2-fold enrichment of NR2F1-positive tumor cells in close proximity (0-40m) to macrophages in primary tumors (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig7">7b</a>). However, to confirm that the induction of NR2F1 is due to macrophage-tumor cell and not macrophage-endothelial cell interactions, we co-cultured tumor cells with macrophages or with endothelial cells and stained them for NR2F1. We found that NR2F1 expression is significantly increased in tumor cells co-cultured with macrophages compared to tumor cells cultured alone (48% <i>vs</i>. 10%), or co-cultured with endothelial cells (16% <i>vs</i>. 10%) (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig7">7c, d</a>). When co-cultured cells were separated by a 0.4m porous membrane, we observed a similar increase in tumor cell NR2F1 expression in the presence of macrophages, indicating that soluble factors are responsible for the induction of NR2F1 (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">8a, b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Macrophages regulate dormancy in tumor cells."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 7: Macrophages regulate dormancy in tumor cells.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig7_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig7" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig7_HTML.png" alt="figure 7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="425"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p><b>a</b> Representative image of triple immunofluorescently stained in E0771-GFP primary tumor tissue for tumor cells, macrophages, and NR2F1. Green=GFP; Red=NR2F1; White=IBA-1; Blue=DAPI. White arrow shows a macrophage. The yellow arrow shows the contact between an NR2F1-positive tumor cell and a macrophage. M=Macrophage. For comparison between the 0 and 200m bins a two-tailed Mann-Whitney test was used (<i>p</i>&lt;0.0001). ****<i>p</i>&lt;0.0001. <b>c</b> Representative immunofluorescence images of NR2F1 expression in E0771-GFP tumor cells cultured alone, in direct contact with BAC1.2F5 macrophages, or in direct contact with HUVEC endothelial cells. White arrows show macrophages or endothelial cells in direct contact with a tumor cell. Green=GFP; Red=NR2F1; Blue=DAPI. TC=Tumor Cell. M=Macrophage. EC=Endothelial Cell. For TC+M vs. TC+EC (0.012), a two-tailed one-way ANOVA with Sidaks multiple comparison adjustment was used. *<i>p</i>&lt;0.05. **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01; ns=not significant. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 7" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To determine the impact that the presence or absence of macrophages, systemically, has on NR2F1 expression, we treated tumor-bearing mice (SM model) with control liposomes or clodronate liposomes to systemically deplete them of macrophages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="van Rooijen, N. &amp; van Kesteren-Hendrikx, E. In vivo depletion of macrophages by liposome-mediated suicide. Methods Enzymol 373, 316 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2174">56</a></sup>. Clodronate treatment led to a significant macrophage depletion in primary tumor tissues (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig8">8a, b</a>, Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">8c, d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="Macrophage depletion reduces dormancy in primary tumors, CTCs, and disseminating tumor cells in vivo."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 8: Macrophage depletion reduces dormancy in primary tumors, CTCs, and disseminating tumor cells in vivo.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig8_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig8" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig8_HTML.png" alt="figure 8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="482"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p><b>a</b> Representative immunofluorescence images of E0771-GFP primary tumor tissues treated with either control liposomes or clodronate liposomes and stained for macrophages: IBA-1=White; DAPI=Blue. Scale bar for Primary Tumor =100 m. M=Macrophage. <b>b</b> Percentage of IBA-1 positive macrophages in 10 fields of view (1088629 m<sup>2</sup>) in each group from Fig. 8a. Control Liposomes: <i>n</i>=60 HPFs in 6 animals. Clodronate liposomes: <i>n</i>=50 HPFs in 5 animals. Bar=mean. Error bars=SEM. Unpaired t-test (<i>p</i>=0.0087). **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01. <b>c</b> Representative immunofluorescence images of NR2F1 expression in primary tumors, circulating tumor cells (CTCs), and disseminated tumor cells (Lung) from an E0771-GFP SM model treated with control (Left) or clodronate liposomes (Right). Green=GFP, Red=NR2F1, Blue=DAPI. Scale bar for Primary Tumor=50m. Scale bar for CTCs and Lung=15 m. <b>d</b> Percentage of NR2F1-positive tumor cells in each group from Fig. 8c. Control Liposomes - Primary Tumor: 3048 cells in 119 fields of view (6565m<sup>2</sup>) in 8 animals; CTCs: <i>n</i>=139 cells in 5 animals; Lung: <i>n</i>=166 cells in 7 animals. Clodronate Liposomes - Primary Tumor: <i>n</i>=2,298 cells in 79 fields of view (6565m<sup>2</sup>) in 6 animals, CTCs: <i>n</i>=293 cells in 6 animals; Lung: <i>n</i>=190 cells in 7 animals. For primary tumor (<i>p</i>=0.007), a Mann-Whitney test was used. For CTCs (<i>p</i>=0.038) and Lungs (<i>p</i>=0.0029), unpaired t-tests were used. *<i>p</i>&lt;0.05. **<i>p</i>&lt;0.01. Source data are provided as a Source Data file.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 8" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We then quantified the percentage of NR2F1-positive tumor cells in primary tumors, CTCs, and lung tissues from an E0771-GFP SM model (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig8">8c</a>). We found that, while the overall number of disseminated tumor cells is reduced in clodronate liposome treated animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Roussos, E. T. et al. Mena invasive (MenaINV) promotes multicellular streaming motility and transendothelial migration in a mouse model of breast cancer. J. Cell Sci. 124, 21202131 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2272">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wyckoff, J. B. et al. Direct visualization of macrophage-assisted tumor cell intravasation in mammary tumors. Cancer Res. 67, 26492656 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2275">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Patsialou, A. et al. Intravital multiphoton imaging reveals multicellular streaming as a crucial component of in vivo cell migration in human breast tumors. Intravital 2, e25294 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2278">57</a></sup>, of the cells that do disseminate, there is a significant reduction in the fraction that are positive for NR2F1 in CTCs and in disseminated tumor cells found in the lungs, as well as in the primary tumor, compared to control animals (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig8">8d</a>). Similar observations were made with 231-GFP cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">8e, f</a>).</p><p>To rule out the possibility that clodronate treatment affects other phagocytic cell types, we analyzed dendritic cells (CD11c<sup>+</sup> and IBA-1<sup></sup>) in both mice treated with liposome control and clodronate liposomes. In accordance with earlier published studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Baert, T. et al. Myeloid Derived Suppressor Cells: Key Drivers of Immunosuppression in Ovarian Cancer. Front. Immunol. 10, 1273 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2295">58</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Buiting, A. M. &amp; Van Rooijen, N. Liposome mediated depletion of macrophages: an approach for fundamental studies. J. Drug Target 2, 357362 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2298">59</a></sup>, we observed no significant change in the number of CD11c<sup>+</sup> IBA-1<sup></sup> cells following clodronate treatment, suggesting that clodronate is specific to macrophages (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>We therefore conclude that tumor associated macrophages in the primary tumor play an important role in inducing the expression of dormancy markers in primary cancer cells that are destined to disseminate.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Expression of NR2F1 and Mena<sup>INV</sup> are regulated together</h3><p>Given our observation that the expression of Mena<sup>INV</sup> and NR2F1 in tumor cells are both increased near TMEM doorways, we sought to determine if both proteins are mechanistically linked, or independently regulated. To this end, we stained and quantified the expression of NR2F1 in MMTV-PyMT tumors taken from wild-type and Mena knock-out mice (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">10a</a>). We found that NR2F1 expression is significantly decreased in mice lacking expression of Mena (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">10b</a>). This indicates that NR2F1 and Mena<sup>INV</sup> are, to some extent, dependently regulated within tumor microenvironment, although we cannot conclude from these data that Mena directly regulates NR2F1. There is still a possibility that they have independent or indirect mechanisms of regulation as well.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Systemic depletion of macrophages reduces tumor cell retention, extravasation, and survival</h3><p>Given that spontaneously DTCs acquire the expression of Mena<sup>INV</sup> and NR2F1 when they interact with macrophages near TMEM doorways, we sought to test whether systemic depletion of macrophages would prevent the ability of tumor cells to extravasate and survive in secondary sites, as is expected based upon earlier work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Qian, B. Z. et al. CCL2 recruits inflammatory monocytes to facilitate breast-tumour metastasis. Nature 475, 222225 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2345">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Qian, B. et al. A distinct macrophage population mediates metastatic breast cancer cell extravasation, establishment and growth. PLoS One 4, e6562 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2348">61</a></sup>. To this end, we systemically depleted macrophages in mice bearing a primary tumor (SM model) using clodronate liposomes, and tracked the fate of DTCs during the steps of retention, extravasation, and survival in the lung (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">11a</a>). As expected, in mice treated with control liposomes, a large percentage of E0771-GFP tumor cells were retained in the lung for the entire experimental period (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">11b</a>). However, in macrophage-depleted mice, the number of tumor cells retained in the lung drastically decreased with only few cells remaining in the lung at the end of the experiment (5%). We further found that, while the majority of tumor cells were able to extravasate in the control mice (68%), a significantly lower proportion of cells (31%) extravasated during the same time period after arrival to the lung in the clodronate treated mice (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">11c</a>). We further aimed to test if the depletion of macrophages would influence tumor cell survival after extravasation in the lung. To this end, we tracked each individual tumor cell longitudinally, as previously described. We found that the depletion of macrophages drastically decreased the survival of DTCs after extravasation, making them similar to EM cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">11d</a>). Together, these data, along with those in Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig7">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig8">8</a>, indicate the crucial role of primary tumor-associated macrophages in educating tumor cells for retention, extravasation, and survival in the lung, although we cannot rule out the possibility that macrophages at secondary sites might play a complementary role.</p><p>To further confirm the role of macrophages in tumor cell extravasation and survival, we used an alternative method to systemically deplete macrophages: the macrophage-fas-induced apoptosis (MaFIA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Harney, A. S. et al. Real-Time Imaging Reveals Local, Transient Vascular Permeability, and Tumor Cell Intravasation Stimulated by TIE2hi Macrophage-Derived VEGFA. Cancer Dis. 5, 932943 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2374">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Clifford, A. B., Elnaggar, A. M., Robison, R. A. &amp; ONeill, K. Investigating the role of macrophages in tumor formation using a MaFIA mouse model. Oncol. Rep. 30, 890896 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2377">62</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Arwert, E. N. et al. A Unidirectional Transition from Migratory to Perivascular Macrophage Is Required for Tumor Cell Intravasation. Cell Rep. 23, 12391248 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2380">63</a></sup>. In this model, treatment with the small molecule, AP20187 (also known as the B/B homodimerizer), drastically decreases the number of macrophages systemically (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">12</a>. Similar observations to the clodronate treatment were made with the MaFIA mice, validating the role of macrophages in tumor cell retention, extravasation, and survival (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Macrophages condition disseminating tumor cells for enhanced retention, extravasation, and survival</h3><p>To further test whether interactions between tumor cells and macrophages before dissemination would educate tumor cells to acquire the ability to be retained and survive in the lung, we co-cultured tumor cells with or without macrophages in vitro, isolated the tumor cells, injected them into the tail veins of mice, and tracked their fate. We found that ~33% of macrophage-conditioned tumor cells were retained in the lung for the entire experimental period compared to only 5% of tumor cells cultured alone (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">14a, b</a>) indicating that tumor cell-macrophage interactions, before intravasation, can educate disseminating tumor cells for increased retention within the nave lung.</p><p>Although this increase in retention was statistically significant, we did not observe as great an increase as was observed with SM cells. Indeed, the difference between the SM cells and the macrophage conditioned tumor cells was also statistically significant, suggesting that macrophages within the secondary site (or other variables not reproduced by the in vitro co-culture) additionally condition disseminating tumor cells for increased retention, as has been described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Qian, B. Z. et al. CCL2 recruits inflammatory monocytes to facilitate breast-tumour metastasis. Nature 475, 222225 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2404">60</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Qian, B. et al. A distinct macrophage population mediates metastatic breast cancer cell extravasation, establishment and growth. PLoS One 4, e6562 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2407">61</a></sup>.</p><p>Similarly, we found that macrophage conditioned tumor cells survived after extravasation at rates similar to SM cells, again indicating that tumor cell-macrophage interactions, before intravasation, play a role in educating tumor cells for retention and post-extravasation survival at the secondary site. Together, these data indicate that interactions with macrophages before arrival to the secondary site increase retention and survival of disseminating tumor cells, although not to the same level as SM cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">The presence of a primary tumor cannot account for all SM phenotypes</h3><p>Given that tumor cell-macrophage interactions before dissemination are not able to completely rescue the SM phenotype, we next asked whether the simple presence of the primary tumor also affects the ability of DTCs to be retained, extravasate, and survive in the secondary site. To address this question, we intravenously injected E0771-GFP tumor cells into mice bearing a primary tumor labeled with a different color (E0771-mCherry), and tracked the fate of the GFP labeled cells for 48 hrs (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">15a</a>). In contrast to the near-complete clearance of EM cells injected into nave mice, EM cells injected into mice bearing primary tumors (EM+PT cells) show a statistically significant increase in retention (8% <i>vs</i>. 37%) over 48 hrs (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">15b</a>). However, EM+PT cells are also retained at significantly lower levels than SM cells, indicating that conditioning of the secondary site by the primary tumor is, by itself, insufficient to rescue the SM phenotype. We further found that EM+PT cells show higher levels of extravasation into the lung parenchyma, approaching those observed with SM cells (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">15c</a>). Finally, we observed that the survival of EM+PT cells after extravasation never reached the levels of SM cells, with the difference from EM not statistically significant (Supplementary Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">15d</a>).</p><p>Taken together, these results suggest that while the presence of the primary tumor only partially influences tumor cell retention and extravasation: the impact is incomplete compared to that observed with SM cells with SM enabling a unique retention and a unique survival capacity post-extravasation. Therefore, as a whole, the primary tumors effect on the systemic environment cannot completely account for the SM phenotypes.</p></div></div></section><section data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec18-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec18">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec18-content"><p>Studying the fate of individual DTCs in an intact organ such as the lung has been a major challenge for metastasis research because, until recently, it has not been possible to follow individual DTCs in the lung over time. The result of this limitation is that it has been impossible to determine when spontaneously disseminated tumor cells had arrived to the organ, and for how long they had resided there. To overcome this limitation many studies have relied on the experimental metastasis (EM) assay where tumor cells are injected directly into the vasculature to set a time zero for the study.</p><p>As revealed in our work, a major caveat of the EM assay is the implicit assumption that the processes of education that disseminating tumor cells undergo within the primary lesion are of marginal importance for DTC fate, and that cancer cells injected as a bolus directly into the vasculature are identical in their ability to progress through the metastatic cascade.</p><p>However, it is becoming increasingly clear that the primary tumor plays an important role in determining DTC fate. For example, it is possible to find gene signatures within the primary tumor that indicate whether tumor cells are likely to disseminate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Patsialou, A. et al. Selective gene-expression profiling of migratory tumor cells in vivo predicts clinical outcome in breast cancer patients. Breast Cancer Res. 14, R139 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2455">64</a></sup>, and if they are likely to grow into metastases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Paik, S. et al. A multigene assay to predict recurrence of tamoxifen-treated, node-negative breast cancer. N. Engl. J. Med. 351, 28172826 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2459">65</a></sup>, even long after dissemination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Cheng, Q. et al. A signature of epithelial-mesenchymal plasticity and stromal activation in primary tumor modulates late recurrence in breast cancer independent of disease subtype. Breast Cancer Res. 16, 407 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2463">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS One 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2466">20</a></sup>. In models where primary tumors are never excised, these can create systemic effects, preparing pre-metastatic niches in secondary sites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Hoshino, A. et al. Tumour exosome integrins determine organotropic metastasis. Nature 527, 329335 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2470">66</a></sup> or influencing the reaction of the immune system to disseminated tumor cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Piranlioglu, R. et al. Primary tumor-induced immunity eradicates disseminated tumor cells in syngeneic mouse model. Nat. Commun. 10, 1430 (2019)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2474">67</a></sup>. In addition to these effects, we recently determined that intratumoral microenvironments can activate programs of dormancy in DTCs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2479">21</a></sup>, which may be the unaccounted for mechanism of therapy evasion and late recurrence in patients.</p><p>This is in accord with our observation that tumor cells that spontaneously disseminate from primary tumors remain in the lung for extended periods of time compared to those that are injected directly into the vasculature. Thus, the primary tumor microenvironment and access to vasculature from these niches play a protective role for the cancer cells destined to disseminate.</p><p>It was previously proposed that the harsh conditions of the circulatory system lead to tumor cell destruction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Weiss, L., Nannmark, U., Johansson, B. R. &amp; Bagge, U. Lethal deformation of cancer cells in the microcirculation: a potential rate regulator of hematogenous metastasis. Int. J. Cancer 50, 103107 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2490">33</a></sup>, and that metastatic seeding is more likely to occur in areas with low perfusion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Follain, G. et al. Hemodynamic Forces Tune the Arrest, Adhesion, and Extravasation of Circulating Tumor Cells. Dev. Cell 45, 3352 e12 (2018)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2494">27</a></sup>. Consistent with this, we found that the survival advantage of spontaneously metastasizing (SM) cells over injected cells may be connected to their ability to extravasate into the lung parenchyma faster, resulting in a decreased time-from-arrival-to-extravasation and a shorter exposure to the circulatory system. Clearance of EM tumor cells from the vasculature could not be a result of an adaptive immune reaction to GFP, firstly, because it has been previously shown that GFP produces no detectable in vivo immune responses in C57B6 mice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Skelton, D., Satake, N. &amp; Kohn, D. B. The enhanced green fluorescent protein (eGFP) is minimally immunogenic in C57BL/6 mice. Gene. Ther. 8, 18131814 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2498">68</a></sup>, and second, because any immune reaction would be expected to affect both EM and SM models equally, making EM and SM cells equally susceptible to adaptive immune clearance. Taken together, these observations indicate that neither destruction in the circulation nor extravasation are major limiting steps for disseminating tumor cells originating in a primary tumor.</p><p>Our observations show that, although the difference in overall tumor cell retention between EM and SM cells in the immunocompromised model is significant, it is not as large as that observed in a syngeneic model (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig1">1c</a>). This reduction could be due to the immune system. While it has not been shown that B and T cells influence DTC clearance from the lung vasculature, it has been demonstrated that NK cells play a critical role in preventing tumor cell retention in the lung<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Spiegel, A. et al. Neutrophils Suppress Intraluminal NK Cell-Mediated Tumor Cell Clearance and Enhance Extravasation of Disseminated Carcinoma Cells. Cancer Discov. 6, 630649 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2508">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Barlozzari, T., Reynolds, C. W. &amp; Herberman, R. B. In vivo role of natural killer cells: involvement of large granular lymphocytes in the clearance of tumor cells in anti-asialo GM1-treated rats. J. Immunol. 131, 10241027 (1983)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2511">69</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Riccardi, C., Santoni, A., Barlozzari, T., Puccetti, P. &amp; Herberman, R. B. In vivo natural reactivity of mice against tumor cells. Int. J. Cancer 25, 475486 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2514">70</a></sup> and several studies have demonstrated that the activity of NK cells is higher in Nude mice compared to C57B6 mice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Hanna, N. Expression of metastatic potential of tumor cells in young nude mice is correlated with low levels of natural killer cell-mediated cytotoxicity. Int. J. Cancer 26, 675680 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2518">71</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Gorelik, E., Wiltrout, R. H., Okumura, K., Habu, S. &amp; Herberman, R. B. Role of NK cells in the control of metastatic spread and growth of tumor cells in mice. Int. J. Cancer 30, 107112 (1982)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2521">72</a></sup>. It has further been shown that there is a difference in NK cell reactivity between Nude and C57B6 mice, with Nude mice having much higher levels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Hanna, N. Expression of metastatic potential of tumor cells in young nude mice is correlated with low levels of natural killer cell-mediated cytotoxicity. Int. J. Cancer 26, 675680 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2525">71</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Hanna, N. &amp; Fidler, I. J. Role of natural killer cells in the destruction of circulating tumor emboli. J. Natl. Cancer Inst. 65, 801809 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2528">73</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Talmadge, J. E., Meyers, K. M., Prieur, D. J. &amp; Starkey, J. R. Role of NK cells in tumour growth and metastasis in beige mice. Nature 284, 622624 (1980)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2531">74</a></sup>. Therefore, we propose that the observed increased clearance in the 231-GFP SM model is likely due to a higher activity of NK cells in Nude mice compared to C57B6 mice. This is an avenue that merits further investigation.</p><p>Through gain- and loss-of-function experiments, we showed that Mena<sup>INV</sup> (an isoform of the actin regulatory protein, Mena, involved in cell motility and chemotaxis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Gertler, F. &amp; Condeelis, J. Metastasis: tumor cells becoming MENAcing. Trends Cell Biol. 21, 8190 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2540">38</a></sup>), is required for extravasation and is one important factor in the faster extravasation of SM cells compared to EM cells. We previously showed that expression of Mena<sup>INV</sup> drives invadopodium assembly and function<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Macrophage-dependent tumor cell transendothelial migration is mediated by Notch1/MenaINV-initiated invadopodium formation. Sci. Rep. 6, 37874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2546">35</a></sup> and is required for transendothelial migration within the primary tumor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Invasive breast carcinoma cells from patients exhibit MenaINV- and macrophage-dependent transendothelial migration. Sci. Signal 7, ra112 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2550">75</a></sup>, and that expression of Mena<sup>INV</sup> persists in primary tumors, CTCs, and DTCs in the lung<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Goswami, S. et al. Identification of invasion specific splice variants of the cytoskeletal protein Mena present in mammary tumor cells during invasion in vivo. Clin. Exp. Metastasis 26, 153159 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2557">76</a></sup>. However, it was unknown whether Mena<sup>INV</sup> plays a role in extravasation in the secondary site. Our current work thus indicates that Mena<sup>INV</sup> is a common molecular pathway, important for many of the steps of metastasis including invasion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Macrophage-dependent tumor cell transendothelial migration is mediated by Notch1/MenaINV-initiated invadopodium formation. Sci. Rep. 6, 37874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2565">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Gertler, F. &amp; Condeelis, J. Metastasis: tumor cells becoming MENAcing. Trends Cell Biol. 21, 8190 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2568">38</a></sup>, intravasation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Invasive breast carcinoma cells from patients exhibit MenaINV- and macrophage-dependent transendothelial migration. Sci. Signal 7, ra112 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2572">75</a></sup>, and now extravasation (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig9">9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-9" data-title="Model illustrating how the presence of a primary tumor programs disseminated tumor cells for stemness and dormancy at the secondary site. Left Panel: Within the primary tumor, migrating tumor cells are attracted to blood vessels."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig9" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 9: Model illustrating how the presence of a primary tumor programs disseminated tumor cells for stemness and dormancy at the secondary site. Left Panel: Within the primary tumor, migrating tumor cells are attracted to blood vessels.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/9" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig9_HTML.png?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="Fig9" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-022-28076-3/MediaObjects/41467_2022_28076_Fig9_HTML.png" alt="figure 9" loading="lazy" width="685" height="350"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-9-desc"><p>As they approach TMEM doorways (red triangle) on the vasculature, these tumor cells interact with macrophages and programs of dormancy (NR2F1) and invasion (Mena<sup>INV</sup>) are activated. Dormant cells also adopt cancer stem cell properties (SOX9). These cells then intravasate through TMEM doorways into the vasculature and become circulating tumor cells (CTCs). <b>Right Panel:</b> CTCs retain these programs at the secondary site where the invasion program (Mena<sup>INV</sup>) facilitates extravasation. The dormancy program expressed by these disseminated tumor cells (DTCs) keeps them as single cells.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3/figures/9" data-track-dest="link:Figure9 Full size image" aria-label="Full size image figure 9" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" focusable="false" role="img" aria-hidden="true" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Given the importance of Mena<sup>INV</sup> for intravasation, it may be surprising that only ~50% of DTCs in the lung show Mena<sup>INV</sup> expression (Fig.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#Fig2">2b</a>, Supplementary Figs.<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">2</a>e and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#MOESM1">7b</a>). However, the expression of this protein is highly transient, with a lifetime on the order of only 2min<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Philippar, U. et al. A Mena invasion isoform potentiates EGF-induced carcinoma cell invasion and metastasis. Dev. Cell 15, 813828 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2624">37</a></sup>. Thus, it is to be expected that, without a sustaining signal, much of this expression would be lost as it might not be needed for post-extravasation survival.</p><p>Our prior work has shown that Mena<sup>INV</sup> is induced via macrophage-tumor cell contact, and that this induction is mediated by Notch signaling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Pignatelli, J. et al. Macrophage-dependent tumor cell transendothelial migration is mediated by Notch1/MenaINV-initiated invadopodium formation. Sci. Rep. 6, 37874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2633">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Eddy, R. J., Weidmann, M. D., Sharma, V. P. &amp; Condeelis, J. S. Tumor Cell Invadopodia: Invasive Protrusions that Orchestrate Metastasis. Trends Cell Biol. 27, 595607 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2636">77</a></sup>. Though it was previously reported that no decrease in metastatic efficiency is observed after specific ablation of alveolar macrophages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Qian, B. et al. A distinct macrophage population mediates metastatic breast cancer cell extravasation, establishment and growth. PLoS One 4, e6562 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2640">61</a></sup>, it is important to note that this observation was made using an EM model where the secondary site was not conditioned by the primary tumor. Further, the alveolar macrophage function may be cancer-type regulated as it was recently shown that it is required for the initial steps of lung cancer lung colonization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Casanova-Acebes, M. et al. Tissue-resident macrophages provide a pro-tumorigenic niche to early NSCLC cells. Nature 595, 578584 (2021)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2644">78</a></sup>. However, further study of alveolar macrophage function is needed to rule in or out their role in SM biology</p><p>A distinguishing feature between EM and SM models is that the vast majority of SM cells do survive in the lung as solitary tumor cells, suggesting that they may be in a dormant state. Indeed, we found that, as in other studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2651">21</a></sup>, DTCs express nuclear NR2F1, a well-established marker for dormancy used in different pre-clinical models<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Kim, R. S. et al. Dormancy signatures and metastasis in estrogen receptor positive and negative breast cancer. PLoS One 7, e35569 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2655">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Fluegen, G. et al. Phenotypic heterogeneity of disseminated tumour cells is preset by primary tumour hypoxic microenvironments. Nat. Cell Biol. 19, 120132 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-022-28076-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d42385980e2658">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Sosa, M.
